Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_478_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]EALT: The Roman Republic was ruled by a military and religious aristocracy which maintained an extremely complex set of religious and political institutions that with no written constitution[MAJIDBENAM_REF_479_ItRomMR] significantly evolved over the 509-264 BCE period in the aftermath of the monarchy (509-367 BCE) and then as the city-state of Rome conquered and began to rule foreign peoples and territories. The aristocracy used voting assemblies to elect two ruling officials called magesteries who appointed an aristocratic assembly known as the Senate which instructed or advised (in the form of decrees) the executive. Magistrates had "executive, judicial, legislative, diplomatic, military and even religious functions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_480_ItRomMR] Consuls, elected by the comitia centuriata (assembly of military), were "commanders of military forces ... [who] presided over meetings of the Senate and implemented its decisions." They "were always patrician until 367 BC, when plebeians could stand for office"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_481_ItRomMR] and could appoint a single dictator for a six-month period for times of crisis.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_482_ItRomMR] Consuls always acted in collaboration with the senate.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_483_ItRomMR] For Roman law-making the voting assembly comitia centuriata was the "main legislative body [which] passed laws (leges) named after the magistrates who proposed them. After 287 BC the people controlled legislation with their resolutions (plebiscita). The comitia centuriata rarely legislated after 218 BC."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_484_ItRomMR]; additionally, "edicts of magistrates with imperium and interpretations of jurists ... had the force of law", whilst "Senatorial decrees (senatus consulta ...)... were strictly only advice to magistrates but were generally binding"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_485_ItRomMR]. "As Rome expanded, more magistrates were needed, and the consuls lost some of their original functions to other magistrates."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_486_ItRomMR] From the 318 BCE plebiscitum Ovinium, censors, two magesteries elected by the comitia centuriata who had "especially important sacral and civic duties",[MAJIDBENAM_REF_487_ItRomMR] replaced the two consuls as the individuals with the authority to appoint and remove senators, and they also appointed new consuls as senators. The Roman magesteries reflect both the aristocratic nature of Roman political organization which could permit powerful individuals but did not enable a single individual to dominate Roman society: "The term of office for magistrates was one year, and a senator would expect to be elected to at least two or three magistracies during his lifetime. A magistrate could hold other offices simultaneously, such as a priesthood."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_488_ItRomMR] It is speculated that the three ruling tribes or families (patricians) that ruled the Roman commoners (plebeians) during the regal period, before the tyranny, may have again had political significance during the historically murkier 509-367 BCE period; in that the aristocratic rulers may have wished to create a political system whereby one family could never dominate.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_489_ItRomMR] In the consulship "no two individuals of the same gens ever held office contemporaneously" but in the brief consular tribunate "there [were] instances of the same gens holding more than one office."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_490_ItRomMR] "There is, in other words, no instance where one gens was able to dominate in any year, and we know that laws were passed later in the republic to insist on distribution of office in the priesthood on exactly these grounds."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_491_ItRomMR] In this respect, perhaps, it may be interesting that the Roman Republic had no independent central bureaucracy; it was not until the empire period that revenues were diverted into an imperial treasury[MAJIDBENAM_REF_492_ItRomMR]. The state treasury - cash and Senate archives - was in fact under the custody of a powerful priesthood inside the temple of Saturn controlled by elected officials called quaestors[MAJIDBENAM_REF_493_ItRomMR]. The Pontifex Maximus, chief priest of the college of pontifices, was also an elected position - elected by the comitia tributa (assembly of all citizens). The pontifice lead the state cult and had to "maintain the pax deorum, the 'peace with the gods'"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_494_ItRomMR]. The location of the Roman treasury was thus a symbolic keystone for the Roman Republic because the all-important public auspices rituals "formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_495_ItRomMR]; in theory held by the executive and first consuls from 509 CE,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_496_ItRomMR] in practice managed by specialist elected priests and full-time priestesses (Vestals, supported by the state and "full-time professional clergy, along with the priestess of Ceres and Proserpina"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_497_ItRomMR]) and an array of religious institutions that determined what the Roman gods wanted (e.g. college of augurs) and attempted to pleased them through ritual sacrifices and maintainence of their temples (e.g. vestals and flamines). Religious offices (duum viri) also were dedicated to interpreting sacred texts known as the Libri Sibyllini that the Romans considered prophetic (retained from the regal period). Romans considered infrastructure dedicated to reading omens and interpreting divine will necessary because every major action of the Roman state had to be legitimated by a reading of the public auspices (the signs of Jupiter).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_498_ItRomMR]. This gave the Roman priesthood an important influence over Roman society. Until Lex Ogulnia (300 BCE) all pontifices were patricians.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_499_ItRomMR] However, the direct role of the priesthood in the affairs of Roman society gradually declined through the 509-264 BCE period: the priests "originally interpreted civil law, ... this role was taken over by jurists (mainly of senatorial rank), and praetors [who] administered the law and issued edicts."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_500_ItRomMR] The famous Twelve Tables of 451-450 BCE was "drawn up by a board of ten men (decemviri legibusscribundis ...)".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_501_ItRomMR] An important trend in the nature of Roman government in the Early Republic was the rise of the plebeian class to positions of power and status. This particularly accellerated from about 444 BCE when began a period historians have called the "Conflict of the Orders". The tribuni militum consulari potestate (consular tribunate) emerged briefly during this time of disruption (to be abolished 367 BCE). "Between 444 and 392, consuls were often replaced by three, four, or six military tribunes with consular power (consular tribunes) and in all years between 391 and 367 by six consular tribunes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_502_ItRomMR] This was an elected office that could be held both by members of the powerful families and the Roman citizens.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_503_ItRomMR] "Here the Romans devised a college of up to six magistrates who had the consular 'power' to lead an army yet who did not have imperium and whose auspices were deficient in some way. The idea perhaps was to keep members of the plebeian class - who were eligible for the office - away from the highest public auspices."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_504_ItRomMR] Nevertheless, after the consular tribunate was abolished by the lex Licinia Sextia "and the consular constitution was reestablished for 366; henceforth plebeians were eligible for election to one of the consulships, and in more than half the years between 366 and 342 a plebeian was elected."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_505_ItRomMR] Due to the murky nature of the sources, however, we can only be certain of much of what we know about the structure of Roman rule such as "a double magistry and a conflict between ruling classes (patrcians) and Roman citizens (plebeians)" from about 367 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_506_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]
Institutions of the Roman Republic[MAJIDBENAM_BR] c270 BCE "most of the classic institutions of the middle Republic, as described by Polybius in the second century, were in place."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_507_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] the most recent best sources of information about them: Consulate, Senate (Bonnefond-Coudry 1989, Ryan 1998), popular assemblies (Farrell 1986, Yakobson 1999, Pina Polo 1989, 1996, Laser 1997, Millar 1998, Sandberg 2001, Morstein-Marx 2004), aedileship (Sabbatucci 1954), tribunate of the plebs (Lobrano 1982, Sancho 1984, Thommen 1989), censorship (Astin 1982, 1985), dictatorship (Hartfield 1982, Hurlet 1993), praetorship (Brennan 2000).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_508_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]comitia centuriata (Centuriate Assembly)[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Originally the army. "Under a consul (or, theoretically, a praetor), the assembly passed important legislative bills (rogationes) into law (lex, plural leges) and voted on war and peace). Under a consul (or dictator, interrex, or consular tribune), it elected consuls, praetors, and censors. Under a consul or praetor (after the assembly was convened, 'lending' auspices to the tribune of the plebs), it conducted popular trials if the penalty was death."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_509_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Comitia curiata (Curiate Assembly).[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "gave 'military auspices' to consuls and to praetors once they were elected by the centuriate assembly, and also to dictators and nonmagistrates cum imperio."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_510_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]comitia tributa - Tribunes and concilium plebis[MAJIDBENAM_BR] - assembly of all citizens. divided into voting districts. In the Regal period the king's power was his 'imperium' and in theory this enabled him to do whatever he wished. In practice, he consulted with a consilium (advisory body of aristocrats) which later became the 'senate'. According to tradition the king "also sought the (well-organised) approval of the people in assembly (populus)"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_511_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] comitia tributa. Tribal Assembly. "After 241 B.C., there were four tribes in Rome itself and thirty-one around the city. New territory, as added, was incorporated administratively into existing tribal units; freedmen, however, were restricted to the four urban tribes. Consuls and praetors presided in this assembly, where individuals had equal votes within their tribes and a majority of tribes determined decisions. The assembly voted on legislation, elected minor magistrates, and conducted some popular trials if the penalty was a fine. Under the lex Domitia of 104 B.C. (suspended 81-63 B.C.), seventeen tribes chosen by lot elected members of the principal colleges of priests."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_512_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] From 494 BCE, after a protest/strike against debt-bondage (nexum),[MAJIDBENAM_REF_513_ItRomMR] Roman citizens were permitted to elect two leaders called tribunes (their number later increased to 10).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_514_ItRomMR] and have an assembly known as the concilium plebis.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_515_ItRomMR] This citizen assembly was able to try the ruling families for crimes against ordinary Romans.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_516_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Concilium Plebis or Comitia Tributa - "the people organized by tribus, areas of domicile." tribunes of the plebs - top plebeian official. "The tribunes became for all practical purposes officials of the Roman state."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_517_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Senate[MAJIDBENAM_BR] members appointed by consuls (by censors from the 318 BCE plebiscitum Ovinium, who also appointed new consuls to senate) and could be removed/impeached by the same official. The senate was an institution which advised the magistrates.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_518_ItRomMR] 300 members before 81 BCE, 600 until 45 BCE, then 900 until[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Augustus reduced it to 600.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_519_ItRomMR] What the senate decided (senatus consultum) in theory only guidance (could be vetoed by a consul or tribune whence it became a patrum auctoritas) but in practice "long guided state administration and policy in almost all matters, including wars, allocation of provinces, (eventually) all extensions of imperium, triumphs, the state religion, and finance; it also engaged in preliminary discussion of legislative bills."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_520_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Quaestors[MAJIDBENAM_BR] elected position. "financial and administrative officials who maintained public records, administered the treasury (aerarium), acted as paymasters accompanying generals on campaigns and were financial secretaries to governors."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_521_ItRomMR] In 2nd century BCE the quaestorship (a provincial appointment?) "was an entry-level office; it had limited powers, and in this period was usually held around age 30."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_522_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Censors[MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Before 387 B.C., when the territory of recently conquered Veii was annexed and formed into four new tribes (Livy 6.5.8), Roman territory had been divided into twenty-one tribes. The city itself formed four urban tribes, and the surrounding countryside was organized into seventeen rustic tribes. Membership of these geographical tribes was assigned by censors. Although the office of censor was created in 443 B.C., it was not until the late fourth century B.C. that the Romans began to elect censors regularly about every five years. During the early history of the office (443-38 B.C.), censors were elected on average about every nine years (Austin 1982). The censors were always two in number and held office for eighteen months. Their primary duty was to conduct a census of the Roman people, which involved taking statements under oath from adult male heads of households as to the members of their family and their property holdings; on the basis of these formal declarations, the censors assigned every adult male to a tribe, to an economic class, and to a place within the comitia centurata, and every household's taxes to the state were calculated according to the assessed value of its property. This entire nexus of the comitia centuriata, census, tribal organization, and state taxation was attributed to King Servius Tullius by the ancient tradition."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_523_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] elected position. Two magistrates "that involved some especially important sacral and civic duties".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_524_ItRomMR] The office of the censor (censorship) from 443 BCE but not always present were two officials who enrolled citizens into military service.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_525_ItRomMR] In 351 BCE the first censors was elected from outside the ruling families.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_526_ItRomMR] c339 BCE lex Publilia ruled that one censor should be a plebeian.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_527_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Aediles[MAJIDBENAM_BR] elected by comitia tributa. "Two plebeian magistrates administered temple of Ceres, function later "extended to public buildings and archives (of the plebiscita and senatus consulta). From 367 BC two curule aediles were elected from the patricians. The plebeian and curule aediles had similar functions at Rome; they were in charge of the maintenance and repair of public buildings (such as temples, roads and aqueducts), of markets (especially weights and measures), of the annona (to the time of Julius Caesar), and of public games and festivals (to the time of Augustus, when games were transferred to praetors)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_528_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Praetor[MAJIDBENAM_BR] elected by comitia centuriata. "In 366 BC the praetor urbanus (city praetor) was introduced, who was almost exclusively concerned with the administration of law at Rome. The praetor ... was the supreme civil judge. By the middle republic the praetors' powers were restricted to law and justice... By 241 BC a second praetor ... was established to deal with legal cases in which one or both parties were foreigners. ... there were eight by 80 BC. Praetors issued annual edicts that were an important source of Roman law."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_529_ItRomMR] "A third magistracy, the praetorship, was also established in 367."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_530_ItRomMR] The first plebeian praetor was in 336 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_531_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Consuls[MAJIDBENAM_BR] elected by the comitia centuriata. "commanders of military forces ... presided over meetings of the Senate and implemented its decisions." They "were always patrician until 367 BC, when plebeians could stand for office." Their minimum age "increased to 42 in the 1st century BC)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_532_ItRomMR] A praetor maximus might have been a principal magistrate "at the head of the newly born republic."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_533_ItRomMR] According to Alexander Bergk "the three chief magistracies created in the year 367, two consuls and one praetor, were colleagues. When wars took place increasingly far away from Rome, the permanent presence of at least one magistrate with imperium in Rome was required. The praetor then lost his military function and the city of Rome was his usual field of action. Only then did the hierarchy of consuls and praetors emerge, and the cursus honorum was born."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_534_ItRomMR] Polybius described consuls as supreme commanders of the Roman army[MAJIDBENAM_REF_535_ItRomMR] but also "the consuls carried out a great number of civil tasks, mainly, but not only, during their stay in Rome at the beginning of the consular year after taking office, a stay that could last a few weeks or months depending on the changing circumstances of internal and external politics."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_536_ItRomMR] Consuls were also "head of Roman diplomacy ... they had to receive foreign embassies in the Urbs and introduce them, if necessary, in the senate".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_537_ItRomMR] Consuls "had legislative initiative, although the number of consular laws passed during the middle republc was relatively small and unquestionably smaller than that of tribunican laws; on occasion, the consuls were commissioned by the senate to conduct extraordinary investigations the consuls were responsible for and promoted many public works, such as a large number of the temples erected in Rome between the fourth and second centuries, and many roads built in Italy during that period; the consuls were responsible for appointing a dictator; and finally, they presided over the elections".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_538_ItRomMR] Consuls had ritual duties such as "public vows, the expiation of prodigies, and the appointment of the date for the celebration of the Feriae Latinae, as well as presidency over the festival at the Mons Albanus, presidency over the public games, and especially responsible for the proper performance of a ver sacrum."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_539_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]State treasury[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Treasury called aerarium or aerarium Saturni (treasury of Saturn). This was used for "depositing cash and archives of the Roman state and was situated in the temple of Saturn below the Capitol. It was controlled by the quaestors under the general supervision of the Senate."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_540_ItRomMR] This treasury still existed during the empire period when revenues "were increasingly diverted into the fiscus (imperial treasury). The aerarium eventually became the treasury of the city of Rome."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_541_ItRomMR]

EALT: "Although the office of censor was created in 443 B.C., it was not until the late fourth century B.C. that the Romans began to elect censors regularly about every five years. During the early history of the office (443-38 B.C.), censors were elected on average about every nine years (Austin 1982). The censors were always two in number and held office for eighteen months."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_547_ItRomMR]

Cursus honorum: quaestor, aedile "not obligatory", praetor, consul, "and finally" censor."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_548_ItRomMR] "Before starting on a political career, young men were expected to have spent at least ten years in a military post (military tribune or legate ...) or sometimes in a legal career in the courts. Magistracies were then sought, usually in a particular order - the cursus honorum (cours eof honors). This was fixed by law in 180 BC, although not everyone followed the normal career pattern".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_549_ItRomMR] Curule magistrates: praetors, consuls, censors, curule aediles.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_550_ItRomMR]

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ ♥ "as a consequence of Rome's urban development during the sixth century, which involved increased economic activity, a rise in population from growth and incorporation of foreigners as new citizens, and the increase in the territorial extent of the Roman state, a new military organization was introduced to take advantage of these economic, demographic, and geographical changes, and the result was a hoplite phalanx recruited from new territorial districts called tribes."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_554_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] "Before 387 B.C., when the territory of recently conquered Veii was annexed and formed into four new tribes (Livy 6.5.8), Roman territory had been divided into twenty-one tribes. The city itself formed four urban tribes, and the surrounding countryside was organized into seventeen rustic tribes. Membership of these geographical tribes was assigned by censors."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_555_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Colonies:[MAJIDBENAM_BR] Especially from late 4th century Rome established colonies of Latins on the various territories that were less reliable allies e.g. Samnite.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_556_ItRomMR] This spread Latin language and culture throughout Italy.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_557_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Beneventum was established in 268 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_558_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Province: Sicily became Rome's first province in 241 BCE after 1st Punic War
♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥ "In the republic governors of provinces were appointed by the Senate and were originally changed annually. Governors were responsbile for law and order, security, administration of justice and the collection of taxes. As Rome expanded its territory, a consul or praetor could expect to become a provincial governor (both called proconsul) at the end of his term of office, giving him money-making opportunities in the provinces. The imperium of a governor was restricted to his province."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_559_ItRomMR] Governor had a financial secretary (quaestor) and legati advisers ("normally senators chosen by the Senate on advice from the governor"). They also had amici (friends and advisors). The quaestor took over as quaestor pro praetor if the governor died or left the province.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_560_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Bribery ♣ ♥
♠ Abuse of power ♣ widespread ♥ Especially after unification of Italy 272 BCE up until Augustus ended the practice, "Tax farmers were often utilized to collect provincial taxes." Problems with tax farmers, as outlined by A. H. M. Jones (1968) included "Oppression and extortion [which] began very early in the provinces and reached fantastic proportions in the later republic. ... There was usually collusion between the governor and the tax contractors and the senate was too far away to exercise any effective control over either."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_568_ItRomMR] As outlined by A. H. M. Jones (1968) "The other great abuse of the provinces was extensive moneylending at exorbitant rates of interest to the provincial communities, which could not raise enough ready cash to satisfy both the exorbitant demands of the tax contractors and the blackmail levied by the governors."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_569_ItRomMR]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ absent ♥ A formal legal code was first founded in the Twelve Tables of 450-449 BCE. Law thereafter was based on precedent. Our sources of knowledge of Roman law include the forensic speeches of Cicero; the Institutes of Gaius textbook (from 160 CE); and, much later, the sixth century CE Corpus Ius Civilis of Justinian. Wax tablets and papyri (contracts and wills etc.) also provide information on Roman law. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_572_ItRomMR] Professional judges did not exist until the Roman Dominate although at that time their precise role vis-a-vis that of Imperial officials is a matter of debate. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_573_ItRomMR] Before this time there were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The senate "almost automatically accepted the findings of major colleges of priests on public law questions that fell within their competence - and so cumulatively went some way toward shaping the res publica in its pragmatic aspects."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_574_ItRomMR] According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_575_ItRomMR] However, whilst mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_576_ItRomMR]
♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ absent ♥During the Roman Dominate administration of justice was "thoroughly bureaucratized" and "regular courts, special courts were established to deal with particular matters and categories of persons." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_577_ItRomMR] Before this time there was no specialised court building. Courts could be held in the basilicas[MAJIDBENAM_REF_578_ItRomMR] which were introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_579_ItRomMR]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman Forum. The Forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom. According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_580_ItRomMR] However, whilst this held true mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_581_ItRomMR] There were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.
♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ present ♥Magistrates had "executive, judicial, legislative, diplomatic, military and even religious functions."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_582_ItRomMR] including prosecuting criminal violations[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Accessibility of the Legal System
♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ This period saw an expansion of access to the legal system to non-citizens, suggesting that all citizens still retained the right to access to the system that they enjoyed in previous periods. "In the last two to three cents. bc, however, the expansion of Rome's commerce and empire over the Mediterranean world (see imperialism, roman) made it impossible to maintain the exclusiveness of the old civil law. New, informal institutions appeared, which depended on the intention of the parties rather than the observance of external forms. An important example is the class of agreements binding by consent alone (consensual contracts: See contract), which provided a way of enforcing the principal commercial transactions, such as sale, lease, and partnership. These new institutions were open to foreigners and citizens alike (see ius gentium). The form of stipulatio, which could be used to make any lawful agreement legally binding, was also widened so that it was now open to foreigners. Special boards of assessors (recuperatores) had early been set up for disputes with non-citizens, and about 242 bc a special magistrate for matters involving foreigners (praetor peregrinus; see praetor (republic)) was created with jurisdiction over these cases."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_583_ItRomMR] The following text, which was originally the supporting text provided for this "present" code, is in fact only tangentially relevant, and is missing some references, but it is preserved in order to keep a record of the variable's coding history. During the Roman Dominate administration of justice was "thoroughly bureaucratized" and "regular courts, special courts were established to deal with particular matters and categories of persons." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_584_ItRomMR] Before this time there was no specialised court building. Courts could be held in the basilicas[MAJIDBENAM_REF_585_ItRomMR] which were introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_586_ItRomMR]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman Forum. The Forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom. According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_587_ItRomMR] However, whilst this held true mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_588_ItRomMR] There were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.

Courts could be held in the basilicas[MAJIDBENAM_REF_614_ItRomMR] which were introduced by the 3rd Century BCE[MAJIDBENAM_REF_615_ItRomMR]) where a provincial governor could an hold audience or in the Roman Forum. The Forum was a multi-purpose building which had existed since the Roman Kingdom. According to Cicero's 13th Philippic of 43 BCE, legal power was of three types "positive laws (leges), ancestral custom (mos maiorum), and accepted precedent (institua)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_616_ItRomMR] However, whilst this held true mostly true within Rome, outside of Rome there was no "effective oversight" on magistrates.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_617_ItRomMR] There were no judges as a distinct profession in the Roman system of law. Local magistrates dealt with local matters, provincial governors dealt with provincial matters, and the praetors often dealt with cases in Rome. The Roman people could be duly convened as a final court of appeal in cases involving citizens.

Informal Justice[edit]

♠ Non-state judiciary ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Religious judiciary ♣ present ♥The senate "almost automatically accepted the findings of major colleges of priests on public law questions that fell within their competence - and so cumulatively went some way toward shaping the res publica in its pragmatic aspects."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_633_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]From c300 BCE "Although the pontiffs continued to be extremely important as expert interpreters of the law, both before and after the passage of the Ogulnian Law, Cn. Flavius's actions [publication of the legis actiones and the legal calendar] simply removed any lingering pretense that the patrician college of pontiffs exercised a monopolistic control of the civil law. Like the increase in number of priests responsible for consulting the Sibylline Books in 368 B.C., this augmentation resulted from the fact that in Roman aristocratic culture these prominent priesthoods were viewed as being akin to public magistracies and as bestowing similar status and prestige. Roman society was becoming increasingly secularized, Roman religion was coming more and more under the influence of Greek culture, and these two trends were seriously eroding the traditional patrician claim to special expert religious knowledge. High-ranking plebeians, who were peers of the most prominent patricians in every other way, were eager to become members of these exclusive religious clubs and thereby to top off their public careers with the added distinction of a priestly office."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_634_ItRomMR]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ present ♥ “The basis of the Roman economy was farming and land ownership.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_635_ItRomMR]
♠ Usufruct ♣ present ♥ “A clear distinction is drawn between those who own the means of production (self-sufficient peasant smallholders, wealthy property-owners) and those who have to make a living working the property of others (slaves, tenants, wage laborers)” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_636_ItRomMR]
♠ Exclusivity ♣ inferred present ♥ We can infer that exclusivity existed, but communal holdings existed as well. “A clear distinction is drawn between those who own the means of production (self-sufficient peasant smallholders, wealthy property-owners) and those who have to make a living working the property of others (slaves, tenants, wage laborers)” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_637_ItRomMR]“The co-residence of relatives and their sharing of land and other resources diminished the impact of the withdrawal part of adult laborers from peasant farms.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_638_ItRomMR]
♠ Partibility ♣ present ♥ “The advance of Roman power in Italy was accompanied by the seizure and expropriation of land from the defeated. Some was used for foundation or refoundation of cities, i.e. coloniae, some for allocation to individuals, either by gift or sale, the rest was retained as public land, being either formally rented or left for occupation against payments to the community.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_639_ItRomMR]
♠ Lease ♣ present ♥ “A clear distinction is drawn between those who own the means of production (self-sufficient peasant smallholders, wealthy property-owners) and those who have to make a living working the property of others (slaves, tenants, wage laborers)” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_640_ItRomMR] “The advance of Roman power in Italy was accompanied by the seizure and expropriation of land from the defeated. ♠ Lease Rights ♣ absent ♥ No official legal rights for renters until late medieval Italy. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_642_ItRomMR]
♠ Transfer of property ♣ ♥ “The main purpose of a will was to designate somebody as the heir…the heir not only inherited some of all of the deceased person’s property, but they also almost literally assumed the testator’s identity and status. Thus, normally the oldest son was made the heir…An heir not only assumed the property and rights of the testator but also inherited any debts.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_643_ItRomMR]
♠ Use of property ♣ inferred absent ♥ Farming was mainly subsistence farming. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_644_ItRomMR]
♠ Property market ♣ absent ♥ “It was also held that originally members of the populous Romanus were permitted to occupy public land at will and farm it.” “A tradition was also known to these writers, according to which a man was only allowed as much public land as he could cultivate himself. This was associated with the lex Licinia of 367 BC restricting holdings of public land.” “Doubts have been raised about whether this kind of occupation was completely uncontrolled by any magistrate.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_645_ItRomMR]
♠ Other property ownership ♣ present ♥ “Slaves were regarded as property, just like any other object owned by their master.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_646_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Inheritance System
♠ partible inheritance ♣ present ♥ “If in your will you wished to include specific gifts of money or property to people other than the heir, you had to add a line called the legacy in which you described the property or amount of money and the person to whom you wanted it to go.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_647_ItRomMR]
♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ present ♥ “It has been held since the early nineteenth century that the control of property by fathers of families at the time of the Twelve Tables had evolved from a system of ownership by villages (pagi) or clans." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_648_ItRomMR]
♠ matrilineal inheritance ♣ absent ♥ Women could not sell or transfer their land. “The patria potestas extended to questions of life and death, as was recognized by the Laws of the Twelve Tables (ca. 450 B.c.), as well over the sons of the family as over the women; cf. Gaius Institutes of Roman Law ii. 96: 'It should be noted that nothing can be granted in the way of justice to those in potestate, i.e., to slaves, children, and wives; for it is reasonable to conclude that, since these persons can own no property, they are incompetent to claim anything in point of law'...Through this change in the marriage customs, nuptiae solo consensu contrahuntur, the husband had use of the dos only, in his wife's property; and that ceased if the marriage was broken off. In other words, the Roman matron was to all intents and purposes a free agent, controlling her own actions and to some extent her own property; for the control of the gens still prevented her from alienating that either by sale or by testament.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_649_ItRomMR]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by only males ♣ present ♥ “The main purpose of a will was to designate somebody as the heir…the heir not only inherited some of all of the deceased person’s property, but they also almost literally assumed the testator’s identity and status. Thus, normally the oldest son was made the heir…An heir not only assumed the property and rights of the testator but also inherited any debts.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_650_ItRomMR] “If in your will you wished to include specific gifts of money or property to people other than the heir, you had to add a line called the legacy in which you described the property or amount of money and the person to whom you wanted it to go.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_651_ItRomMR]
♠ primogeniture inheritance by males and females ♣ inferred absent ♥ “The main purpose of a will was to designate somebody as the heir…the heir not only inherited some of all of the deceased person’s property, but they also almost literally assumed the testator’s identity and status. Thus, normally the oldest son was made the heir…An heir not only assumed the property and rights of the testator but also inherited any debts.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_652_ItRomMR]
♠ other inheritance practices ♣ present ♥ “As time went on, the Romans became concerned that too many people were leaving their property to persons other than their children.” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_653_ItRomMR]

♠ Non-state expropriation ♣ ♥ “Only exceptionally would a god directly claim a plot of land. That might happen by a lightning strike leaving a visible mark in the soil. The strip of land would be marked off by a miniature fence or boxlike structure bearing the inscription fulgar conditum (‘covered lightening-trace’). The owner would hardly lose more than a square foot” [MAJIDBENAM_REF_655_ItRomMR]

Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage
♠ Monogamy ♣ present ♥ "Roman marriage was monogamous. A full Roman marriage could occur only if both parties were Roman citizens or had been granted conubium."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_656_ItRomMR]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ absent ♥ "Roman marriage was monogamous. A full Roman marriage could occur only if both parties were Roman citizens or had been granted conubium."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_657_ItRomMR]
EALT:From a glossary of Latin legal terms: iustum matrimonium "legal marriage, marriage made in accordance with Roman law. Also called instae nuptiae".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_658_ItRomMR] From a glossary of Latin legal terms: stuprum "illicit sex, punishable under the lex Julia de adulteriis. This included adultery (sexual relations between a married woman and someone other than her husband), as well as sexual relations with an unmarried woman or virgin of respectable status."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_659_ItRomMR] lex Julia refers to 23 BCE "in early Roman law, if a woman cohabited with a man for one full year, she was regarded as having entered the man's manus by a kind of usucapio [taking by use]".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_660_ItRomMR]
♠ Informal Elite Polygamy ♣ inferred present ♥ From a glossary of Latin legal terms: concubinatus "a non-legal but long-term sexual relationship, usually between an unmarried man and a woman of lower status with whom iustum matrimonium was either legally impossible or socially inappropriate".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_661_ItRomMR]
♠ Elite Concubinage ♣ inferred absent ♥ From a glossary of Latin legal terms: concubinatus "a non-legal but long-term sexual relationship, usually between an unmarried man and a woman of lower status with whom iustum matrimonium was either legally impossible or socially inappropriate".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_662_ItRomMR]
♠ Typical number of extramarital partners ♣ ♥Legal rights are different on the basis of:
♠ Gender ♣ yes ♥ "juristic innovation contained in the Twelve Tables ... A woman who was in the manus of her husband was basically in the same legal position as her children, who were under the husband's parternal authority. ... If a woman did not enter the manus of her husband, she remained under the paternal authority of her father until he died, at which time she became legally independent. From the time of the Twelve Tables onwards, women who were not under their father's patria potestas or the manus of their husbands were still required to have a legal guardian (tutor) to assist or oversee their legal transactions. This duty was most often performed by a close male relative ... This institution was termed the perpetual guardianship of women (tutela perpetua mulierum), and the Vestal virgins were the only females in early Rome exempt from it (Gauis Institutes 1.145)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_663_ItRomMR]
♠ Social class ♣ yes ♥ The plebeian class were able to hold top governmental positions in the state e.g. consulships certainly after 367 BCE,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_664_ItRomMR] but were excluded from the top Senatorial positions. Also, in the early republic plebeians were "forbidden to marry patricians".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_665_ItRomMR] "The proletarii at Rome were those citizens placed in the lowest property class who were exempt from military service and tributum and could serve the state only by contributing their children (proles) to it."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_666_ItRomMR]
♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ yes ♥ Non-Roman citizens could be excluded from the vote: Dentatus was a particularly aggressive general who conquered territories and incorporated its populations without giving them the vote.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_667_ItRomMR] "To the astonishment of Greek observers, a slave freed by a Roman citizens became a Roman citizen [and] Roman citizenship came to be available in due course not simply to members of Latin communities, but also to entire Italian peoples."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_668_ItRomMR]

Discrimination[edit]

Discrimination on the basis of gender (include de facto discrimination even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)
♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to both males and females ♣ no ♥ The following refers to the imperial period, but suggests continuity with preceding periods. "Although women continued to be barred from public office and from taking legal action on their own behalf, they gained considerable independence from male authority in their private lives."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_669_ItRomMR] There were full-time priestesses, the Vestals and the priestess of Ceres and Proserpina who were supported by the state.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_670_ItRomMR] The latter roles could not be said to be a bureaucratic position and their powers were not governmental: e.g. for Vestals "Wills and treaties were in their keeping, and they themselves could make a will. They could conduct business in their own name. They could give evidence in court without taking an oath. ... If they accidentally met a criminal on his way to execution, he was spared."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_671_ItRomMR]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to both males and females ♣ no ♥ The following refers to the imperial period, but suggests continuity with preceding periods. "Although women continued to be barred from public office and from taking legal action on their own behalf, they gained considerable independence from male authority in their private lives."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_672_ItRomMR]
♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ yes ♥ "Some girls went to school, others were taught privately at home. They were an essential part of various religious rites. In these they were not silent ciphers but active participants, often needing physical stamina for long processions and mental effort to memorize and rehearse songs and ritual."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_673_ItRomMR]
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ no ♥ Likely very limited in this period. E.g. clear that Vestal priestesses could own property: "Wills and treaties were in their keeping, and they themselves could make a will. They could conduct business in their own name"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_674_ItRomMR]; "they could own and administer their own property".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_675_ItRomMR] However, this was not true for all other Roman women: "in early Roman law, if a woman cohabited with a man for one full year, she was regarded as having entered the man's manus by a kind of usucapio [taking by use]".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_676_ItRomMR] "A woman who was in the manus of her husband was basically in the same legal position as her children, who were under the husband's parternal authority. ... If a woman did not enter the manus of her husband, she remained under the paternal authority of her father until he died, at which time she became legally independent. From the time of the Twelve Tables onwards, women who were not under their father's patria potestas or the manus of their husbands were still required to have a legal guardian (tutor) to assist or oversee their legal transactions. This duty was most often performed by a close male relative ... This institution was termed the perpetual guardianship of women (tutela perpetua mulierum), and the Vestal virgins were the only females in early Rome exempt from it (Gauis Institutes 1.145)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_677_ItRomMR] Overall, given that only a handful of females who were chosen for ritual functions were permitted to legally own their own property without the "perpetual guardianship" of a man it does not seem appropriate to code that property could be owned by women.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

"In 300, plebeians won the right to hold places in two of Rome's priestly colleges (the pontiffs and the augurs), both of which were enlarged."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_679_ItRomMR] Plebeians became pontiffs and augurs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_680_ItRomMR] "In the early republic [plebeians] were excluded from the Senate and important priesthoods and had no part in the administration of the law."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_681_ItRomMR]
♠ Promotion in administrative positions is open to people from all social classes ♣ yes ♥ In 367, Lex Licinia Sextia passed: plebeian class were now able to hold top governmental positions in the state e.g. one of the consulships.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_682_ItRomMR] giving plebeians more rights including holding one consulship. ♠ Education is available to people from all social classes ♣ no ♥ "The middle and lower classes' ideals and educational concepts in republican times remain blurry (though, cf. Horsfall 2003: 48–63). We may be sure that usually children of low social contexts did not learn reading and writing at all."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_686_ItRomMR] The following refers to the Principate period: "Most of the population, if they had any formal education at all (levels of illiteracy were staggeringly high) would simply attend a school of letters and obtain basic, functional literacy. The elite, however, would be effectively insulated from them at the very outset, by instruction either at home, or in a town school."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_687_ItRomMR] Education was available primarily for those who could afford it. The upwards mobility of the Roman plebeian class suggests they would have received some education. The plebeian class were able to hold top governmental positions in the state e.g. consulships certainly after 367 BCE.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_688_ItRomMR] NB The following quote derives from a source that does not meet the appropriate academic standards, but we are preserving it in order to have a record of the variable's coding history. "The citizens of the early Roman Republic enjoyed an education system similar to ancient Athens. It was voluntary and parents paid tutors or schools directly. There was very little government interference, so a vibrant education free market of tutors, schools, and apprentices developed."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_689_ItRomMR]
♠ Property can be owned by people from all social classes ♣ yes ♥ Plebeian freeholders attested from Early Republic.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_690_ItRomMR] Enslaved people could also, in theory, own property, or so this quote seems to suggest: "Basically, discipline comprised two components: a carrot and a stick (Bradley 1984). The carrot consisted primarily in the prospect of manumission itself, though it also involved the toleration of personal relationships (contubernia) among the slaves (familia), and the granting of separate property (peculium) for use on their own responsibility (Pólay 1967; Flory 1978; Friedl 1996: 75–85; Brinkhof 1978; Zeber 1981; Schumacher 2001a: 239–48, 265–76). The stick was put into concrete form with the withdrawal of these privileges, and rigorous (p. 594) punitive measures, ranging from corporal punishment to cruel execution (Schumacher 2001a: 276–91)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_691_ItRomMR] The following is also worth noting. "To the astonishment of Greek observers, a slave freed by a Roman citizens became a Roman citizen [and] Roman citizenship came to be available in due course not simply to members of Latin communities, but also to entire Italian peoples."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_692_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race
♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ yes ♥ Citizens and non-citizens had different rights, and some groups were initially granted citizenship without the right to vote, but sources do not suggest that any of this had anything to do with ethnicity or race; if anything, the implication is that any community of outsiders, and any enslaved person, regardless of race or ethnicity, could in theory become a citizen, and therefore enjoy the same rights as ethnic Romans, including standing for magistracies. "Roman citizenship came to possess two features which distinguished it from polis citizenship and which later surprised Greek observers: the automatic incorporation of freed slaves of Romans into the Roman citizen body; and the ease with which whole communities of outsiders could be admitted as citizens. By the time Rome faced the invasion of Hannibal in 218 bc, she had a long history of giving citizenship to Italian communities, either with the vote (optimo iure) or without the vote (sine suffragio). (The latter communities, as with Arpinum, the place of origin of C. Marius (1) and Cicero, were usually later granted the vote.) Apart from Roman communities of these two types and allies, socii, Italy also contained numerous Latin communities, whose members shared a number of rights with Romans, one of which was conubium. A child born to two Romans was a Roman; but so was a child born to a Roman father and a mother from a people possessing conubium. (See ius latii, Latini, municipium.)//"All citizens, after the abolition of the ban on conubium between patricians and plebeians, had conubium; they were also liable to tributum and military service. If they had the vote, they were also eligible to stand for magistracies. "[MAJIDBENAM_REF_693_ItRomMR] Previously coded "absent" or "no" on the basis of the fact that non-citizens could be excluded from the vote and from holding positions of authority. However, this claim was only partially referenced (the reference was to a source's mention of how a general named Dentatus did not grant the right to vote to the communities he incorporated into the Roman dominion[MAJIDBENAM_REF_694_ItRomMR]), and, as has been mentioned, the distinction between "citizen" and "non-citizen" does not seem to reflect ethnic discrimination.

♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ yes ♥ Plebeian freeholders attested from Early Republic.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_701_ItRomMR] Enslaved people could also, in theory, own property, or so this quote seems to suggest: "Basically, discipline comprised two components: a carrot and a stick (Bradley 1984). The carrot consisted primarily in the prospect of manumission itself, though it also involved the toleration of personal relationships (contubernia) among the slaves (familia), and the granting of separate property (peculium) for use on their own responsibility (Pólay 1967; Flory 1978; Friedl 1996: 75–85; Brinkhof 1978; Zeber 1981; Schumacher 2001a: 239–48, 265–76). The stick was put into concrete form with the withdrawal of these privileges, and rigorous (p. 594) punitive measures, ranging from corporal punishment to cruel execution (Schumacher 2001a: 276–91)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_702_ItRomMR] The plebeian class contained freed enslaved people and others of non-Roman ethnic/racial group.

Status[edit]

Elite status
♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ "patricians - an elite class that closed their ranks to new members c. 500 B.C., soon after the expulsion of the Tarquins".[MAJIDBENAM_REF_703_ItRomMR] Elite status directly related to the public auspices ritual which "entailed the competence to request, observe, and announce Jupiter's signs regarding an important act and then to complete what was intended. Since auspication preceded every major action taken on the state's behalf, it formed the basis of regal and then, in the Republic, magisterial power."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_704_ItRomMR] Elite positions became open to plebeian class but to a large degree elite status was hereditary. "the ascent of a family to the consulship over several generations was a common enough phenomenon; and a man who ennobled himself and his descendants by being the first of his family to achieve consulship or other high office was known as a novus homo, new man."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_705_ItRomMR] "Until 445 BCE patricians could not marry plebeians, and a free person could not marry a freedman or freedwoman until legistlation under Augustus made it permissible (except for senators)."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_706_ItRomMR]

Slavery[edit]

♠ Proportion of population enslaved ♣ [10-30] ♥ Need to consult Scheidel "The Slave Population of Roman Italy" - slave population may be smaller than commonly suggested.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"The earliest known Roman code relating to slavery is the Twelve Tables, which dates to approxiately 450 B.C. During the republic and early empire, magisterial edicts, imperial orders, and occasionally senatorial decrees affected the structure, application, and evolution of Roman slave law. Though a few legal texts exist from the first two centuries A.D., no formal and comprehensive compilation of law, including laws pertaining to slavery, appeared before the Theodosian Code (Codex Theodosianus) was assembled in A.D. 435."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_727_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Slavery in the Roman Republic was fully institutionalized by 450 BCE, and was incorporated into the Twelve Tables legal code (451-450 BCE). Indeed, close to 700,000 prisoners of war were enslaved between 297 and 167 BCE in the Roman Empire. Altogether, millions of slaves inhabited ancient Rome, claiming 15-25 percent or even 35 percent of its population."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_728_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"In the first century C.E. in Pergamon in Asia Minor, his home city, Galen estimated that there was a slave for every two adult citizens, so 33 percent; but in the rural villages of Egypt, the number was much lower, below 7 percent. Whereas the earlier Italian slave population had not exceeded 10 percent, Keith Hopkins has estimated that there were about two million slaves in the empire, equal to about 35 percent of the full population. There was significant downward mobility in the Roman empire!"[MAJIDBENAM_REF_729_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Hopkins (1978: 9, 102) estimated that by 31 B.C. there were at least two million slaves in Italy, out of a total population of six million."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_730_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"According to Finley (1980), by the end of the Roman Republic, slaves comprised 30-35 percent of the population of Roman Italy, a figure comparable to that for the antebellum American South."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_731_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Slaves were acquired through warfare.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_732_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Mid-4th BCE: "One major change is likely to have been Rome's gradual transformation into a slave-owning society. Incessant and successful warfare over the course of the fourth century must have produced a steady supply of slaves. One clear indication of the growth in chattel slavery in Roman society during this period is a law passed in 357 B.C. which imposed a 5 percent tax (vicesima libertatis) on manumitted slaves (Livy 7.16.7-8). It is worth nothing that this law was passed by the Roman army at Sutrium, the Latin colony on Rome's Etruscan frontier. It suggests that the army ratified this proposal either before or after a military campaign against non-Latin-speaking people, who must have been prime candidates for the Roman slave market."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_733_ItRomMR][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"That Roman agriculture in the late Republic was heavily dependent on the labour of slaves is well known, but it is not always appreciated that this phenomenon has its origin in the fourth century B.C."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_734_ItRomMR]

♠ Prisoners ♣ present ♥ "As for the Romans, Livy states that during the Punic Wars, they got so desperate after their defeat by Hannibal at Cannae that they buried alive two pairs of victims in the Forum Boarium: 'In the meantime, by the direction of the Books of Fate [the Sibylline books of oracles], some unusual sacrifices were offered; amongst others a Gaulish man and woman and a Greek man and woman were buried alive in the Cattle Market, in a place walled in with stone, which even before this time had been defiled with human victims, a sacrifice wholly alien to the Roman spirit.' (The earlier sacrifice occurred in 228 BCE when the Gauls were invading, and a later one apparently occurred ca. 114 after another military defeat in Illyria (see Gary Forsythe, A Critical History of Early Rome, pp 331-2). Pliny the Elder (28.3) also writes: 'Our own age even has seen a man and a woman buried alive in the Ox Market, Greeks by birth, or else natives of some other country with which we were at war at the time. The prayer used upon the occasion of this ceremonial, and which is usually pronounced first by the Master of the College of the Quindecimviri, if read by a person, must assuredly force him to admit the potency of [prayer] formulae; when it is recollected that it has been proved to be effectual by the experience of eight hundred and thirty years.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_747_ItRomMR]

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ inferred absent ♥ Republican rulers (Senators) based their power on aristocratic heritage, and their authority was not seen as ordained by gods, although religious / secular authority was indistinct and rulers acted to ensure favour of the gods on the Roman Republic.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]
previous code had = Inferred present, based on the following: "Undoubtedly the most salient feature of Roman republican religion lies in the fact that religious authority and religious institutions were tightly interwoven with political authority and the political institutions of the res publica. [...] There was no separate priestly class in which religious authority was vested, but the same men who made decisions regarding the relationships of the Roman community with other human communities also made the decisions regarding its relationship with the divine community." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_751_ItRomMR] True, but religious/secular power is not the same as having rule legitimated through divine authority
♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ absent ♥ "Ruler worship is not generally considered characteristic of Roman society during the republican period (conventionally 509-31 BC), a period during which the polity developed from a conventional city-state to a regional hegemon and finally a territorial empire controlling the entire Mediterranean basin and its immediate hinterlands. Properly speaking, the cult of the emperors extended from the accession of the first emperor, Octavian/Augustus (conventionally dated to 31 BC.) to the conversion to Christianity of the emperor Constantine in AD 312. framed in these terms, divine kingship in rome is a phenomenon limited to the early empire, commonly termed the Principate." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_752_ItRomMR] However, Woolf also argues that "if the Greek world before Alexander, or rome before Augustus, seem to be worlds without ruler cult, this is in part a result of us defining the latter in a rather narrow form." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_753_ItRomMR]

The Julian gens, for example, claimed descent from Venus via Aeneas and his son Julius." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_754_ItRomMR]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ absent ♥ Slavery was an important social institution throughout Roman history [MAJIDBENAM_REF_761_ItRomMR] political ideology asserted clear distinctions between social classes in terms of power, wealth, and authority
♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from the following quote, dating to the Late Republic--so we are inferring continuity between the Middle Republic and this period. "'Equality,' wrote Cicero, 'is unequal when it does not recognize grades of dignity.' The most dignified citizens were the senators, whose dignity consisted in the public offices they held, the responsibilities they shouldered, and the fortunes they spent to maintain public order. Thus they earned the advantages they enjoyed, not least preferential treatment under the law. Ordinary citizens, for their part, were alert, if also submissive, to the “conflict between certain moral aspirations (justice, for instance) and the social institutions (like the law) which attempt to enshrine them.' [...] Cicero’s criticism of undifferentiated equality reflects the other ancient ideology, which provided for the judicial privileges enjoyed by the Roman elite for centuries. [...] Most of its judicial privileges, and most of our evidence for how the ideology of status affected justice, 'were grounded not in legislative enactment, but in administrative rules, customary practices, and ultimately the social attitudes of the ruling elite.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_762_ItRomMR]

♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ disputed_present_absent ♥ "Romans had a penchant for stressing their special values, qualities, and character. The assertions of leaders and the writings of intellectuals regularly affirmed their distinctiveness. A contrast with other peoples loomed large in the development of a self-perception. The history of Rome had, after all, taken shape in a setting that involved confrontations with other cultures right from the start. [...] Stereotypes abound. Harsh judgments by Roman writers on alien peoples seem common and characteristic." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_764_ItRomMR] At the same time, anyone could be made into a Roman citizen, regardless of ethnicity: "A Roman institution, unique in antiquity, affirms this attitude quite decisively: the admission of freed slaves to the citizenship. The Greeks observed the practice with some astonishment. [...] Extension of citizen privileges to communities and peoples within Italy was generous but not startling. A similar generosity toward slaves stood in a different category. The large majority of slaves acquired in this period came from abroad, prisoners of war reduced to servitude or captives purchased on the slave mart. [...] Whatever the motives, [...] the fact of frequent manumission is notable and meaningful. The ready entrance of freedmen into the citizen body signified a level of comfort with foreigners that was unmatched elsewhere in the classical world. What counts here is not liberality or generosity, but the presumption that citizenship could be shared by those of alien birth. Assimilation to Roman ways sufficed to authorize the award of full civic privileges. Romans evidently did not worry about diluting the purity of the stock. Nor did they balk at the exercise of political rights by those whose roots lay in Spain, Cilicia, or Syria. Numbers cannot be ascertained. But the citizenry plainly swelled substantially through the absorption of former slaves. Augustus eventually put on the brake - but not on racial grounds. The attitude of the Roman Republic is clear enough: its populace could only benefit from the admixture of people from everywhere in the Mediterranean." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_765_ItRomMR]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ absent ♥ "To begin with, until fairly late in Roman history, women lacked individual names in the proper sense. Claudia, Julia, Cornelia, Lucretia, are merely family names with a feminine ending. Sisters had the same name and could be distinguished only by the addition of `the elder' or `the younger', `the first' or `the second', and so on. In the not uncommon case of marriage between paternal cousins, mother and daughter would have the same name, too. No doubt this was very confusing: a welcome confusion, one is tempted to suggest, since nothing could have been easier to eliminate. No great genius was needed to think up the idea of giving every girl a personal name, as was done with boys. It is as if the Romans wished to suggest very pointedly that women were not, or ought not to be, genuine individuals but only fractions of a family. Anonymous and passive fractions at that, for the virtues which were stressed were decorum, chastity, gracefulness, even temper and childbearing. They loved their husbands, to be sure--though we need not believe everything that husbands said when their wives were dead--but as one loves an overlord who is free to seek his pleasures elsewhere and to put an end to the relationship altogether when and if he so chooses. [...] Save for relatively minor exceptions, a woman was always in the power of some man--of her paterfamilias or of her husband or of a guardian. In early times every marriage involved a formal ceremony in which the bride was surrendered to her husband by the paterfamilias: he `gave her away' in the literal sense. Then, when so-called `free' marriages became increasingly common--free from the ancient formalities, that is, not free in the sense that the wife or her husband had made a free choice of partner ± she remained legally in the power of her paterfamilias. [...] It would probably be a safe guess that women of the lower classes were therefore more `emancipated', more equal de facto if not in strict law, more widely accepted as persons in their own right than their richer, more bourgeois, or more aristocratic sisters. This is a common enough phe- nomenon everywhere. No doubt they were freer in all senses ± far less inhibited by legal definitions of marriage or legitimacy, less bound by the double standard of sexual morality." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_766_ItRomMR]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ absent ♥ Romans believed themselves to be superior to other peoples partly because Rome was "more sacred" than other places. "Roman rituals were performed in specific places around the city of Rome in order to protect the city. Some of these places had been considered sacred from time immemorial, while others had gained their status over the years, but each location had its specific ritual that needed to be performed on that spot, and at a specified time of the year. Religion permeated almost every element of both space and time for the inhabitants of Rome, leading the Romans to believe them- selves the most religious of all people and to ascribe their military success to their superior cultivation of the gods (Cic. Har. resp. 19)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_767_ItRomMR]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes rulers and commoners ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the following quote, dating to the Late Republic--so we are inferring continuity between the Middle Republic and this period. "'Equality,' wrote Cicero, 'is unequal when it does not recognize grades of dignity.' The most dignified citizens were the senators, whose dignity consisted in the public offices they held, the responsibilities they shouldered, and the fortunes they spent to maintain public order. Thus they earned the advantages they enjoyed, not least preferential treatment under the law. Ordinary citizens, for their part, were alert, if also submissive, to the “conflict between certain moral aspirations (justice, for instance) and the social institutions (like the law) which attempt to enshrine them.' [...] Cicero’s criticism of undifferentiated equality reflects the other ancient ideology, which provided for the judicial privileges enjoyed by the Roman elite for centuries. [...] Most of its judicial privileges, and most of our evidence for how the ideology of status affected justice, 'were grounded not in legislative enactment, but in administrative rules, customary practices, and ultimately the social attitudes of the ruling elite.'" [MAJIDBENAM_REF_768_ItRomMR]
♠ Ideological thought distinguishes elites and commoners ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred from the following quote, dating to the Late Republic--so we are inferring continuity between the Middle Republic and this period. "'Equality,' wrote Cicero, 'is unequal when it does not recognize grades of dignity.'
